  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_525ae56d-683f-4273-a4d9-f73d4dbd70f5'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17281819670229357155">تاكسي: حواديت المشاوير الذي لاقى نجاحا نقديا وجماهيريا كبيرا وغير متوقع، فأثنى عليه الكثير من الكتاب والنقاد واستضافت مؤلفه عدد من البرامج التلفزيونية مثل العاشرة مساء والبيت بيتك والقاهرة اليوم، ووصفه د. عبد الوهاب المسيري بأنه "عمل إبداعي أصيل ومتعة فكرية حقيقية"، وقال عنه د. جلال أمين أنه من أجمل ما قرأ من كتب في وصف المجتمع المصري كما كتب عنه صفحة كاملة بجريدة المصري ا</span>
  <span id="freeText17281819670229357155" style="display:none">تاكسي: حواديت المشاوير الذي لاقى نجاحا نقديا وجماهيريا كبيرا وغير متوقع، فأثنى عليه الكثير من الكتاب والنقاد واستضافت مؤلفه عدد من البرامج التلفزيونية مثل العاشرة مساء والبيت بيتك والقاهرة اليوم، ووصفه د. عبد الوهاب المسيري بأنه "عمل إبداعي أصيل ومتعة فكرية حقيقية"، وقال عنه د. جلال أمين أنه من أجمل ما قرأ من كتب في وصف المجتمع المصري كما كتب عنه صفحة كاملة بجريدة المصري اليوم. والكتاب عبارة عن حوارات بين الراوي وسائقي التاكسي بالقاهرة يتناولون فيها بصراحة بالغة أوضاع البلاد والسياسة والاقتصاد والتطرف والمظاهرات والجنس وحياتهم وهمومهم الشخصية. كتاب ممتع ومرآة صادقة لفئة لماحة تتعامل مع المجتمع كله. المؤلف خالد الخميسي حاصل على ماجستير في العلوم السياسية من جامعة السوربون، إعلامي ومنتج ومخرج وكاتب سيناريو، له العديد من الدراسات الاجتماعية والسياسية، ويكتب في عدة صحف</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/321809786?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/3961732-ron">Ron</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781906300029" href="/book/show/2091415.Taxi">review of another edition</a>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3961732-ron?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer321809786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11628640109228461376">In conversations with 58 Cairo cab drivers, this entertaining novel is a street-level and street-smart portrait of the City on the Nile. Each chapter is a character sketch, no two alike, though there is a theme that runs through most of them - the near impossibility of making a living driving a taxi in this crowded and chaotic metropolis. Each man has a story to tell, sometimes heartbreaking, sometimes hilarious. Taken together they are a lament for the country's economic problems, the undependa</span>
  <span id="freeText11628640109228461376" style="display:none">In conversations with 58 Cairo cab drivers, this entertaining novel is a street-level and street-smart portrait of the City on the Nile. Each chapter is a character sketch, no two alike, though there is a theme that runs through most of them - the near impossibility of making a living driving a taxi in this crowded and chaotic metropolis. Each man has a story to tell, sometimes heartbreaking, sometimes hilarious. Taken together they are a lament for the country's economic problems, the undependability of government, and the frustrations of dealing with law enforcement and a vast bureaucracy that seems unable to operate without the payment of bribes at every turn.<br /><br />The quality of life, as observed by the men behind the wheel, is on a steady trend downward, and often their personal lives are cause for a yet additional string of grievances. Wives complain; children are intractable. The narrator encounters, cynicism, despair, rage, fatalism, faith finally in a just and protecting God. Meanwhile, relief from chagrin comes in the form of humor, as we listen in on an exchange of political and sexual jokes while drivers wait in a long queue at a petrol station. And on rare occasions there's a man who has achieved a kind of beatific peace with it all. If social and political realities are to be found beyond the limited vision of news coverage, this is the place to look. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11628640109228461376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14824045865282709827">Taxi is a collection of over 50 descriptions of fictional encounters with taxi drivers in Cairo. It was published originally in Arabic in 2006 and was a bestseller in Egypt. Imagine the kinds of conversations you might have with a taxi driver, here in Canada. Depending on the news and sports cycles, you might be chatting about an upcoming election and how all the candidates are about the same anyway, or perhaps you might discuss how the price of gas is always going up just before the long weeken</span>
  <span id="freeText14824045865282709827" style="display:none">Taxi is a collection of over 50 descriptions of fictional encounters with taxi drivers in Cairo. It was published originally in Arabic in 2006 and was a bestseller in Egypt. Imagine the kinds of conversations you might have with a taxi driver, here in Canada. Depending on the news and sports cycles, you might be chatting about an upcoming election and how all the candidates are about the same anyway, or perhaps you might discuss how the price of gas is always going up just before the long weekend. The Cairo taxi drivers' conversations are also about those things that are affecting their everyday lives. Their complaints and frustrations with the corruption and poverty give us a glimpse of the foundations of this year's 'Arab Spring'. Fascinating.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3062105035?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Asha Wadhwana " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Asha Wadhwana " href="/user/show/43334376-asha-wadhwana">Asha Wadhwana </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789992178713" href="/book/show/11740407-taxi">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3062105035" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18404915575560128935">I read this book on the recommendation of my cousin who is yet to read this one. <br/>The book is a monologue of 58 taxi drives and the snippet of conversations that occur during that drive. Some of them are relatable, some sad truths of life, some reality of parents in middle class struggling to make ends meet and most of them seemed focused on political bashing (affectionately called as 'Political satire' by my buddy). I was missing some sense of positivity in the stories, I would have loved if the</span>
  <span id="freeText18404915575560128935" style="display:none">I read this book on the recommendation of my cousin who is yet to read this one. <br />The book is a monologue of 58 taxi drives and the snippet of conversations that occur during that drive. Some of them are relatable, some sad truths of life, some reality of parents in middle class struggling to make ends meet and most of them seemed focused on political bashing (affectionately called as 'Political satire' by my buddy). I was missing some sense of positivity in the stories, I would have loved if there was even 10% of positive versions vs the 90% of people voicing their problems. <br /><br />Having had my share of taxi rides in the Gulf, I can vouch with a large amount of certainty that not all taxi drivers are out there to cry about their problems, many of them love to share their achievements too, no matter how big or small, I was missing this balance in the narration and hence it became a task for me to just finish the book, finish what i started. <br /><br />The book however has a very unique way of portraying Egypt to someone who has never visited the place, and the daily struggles narrated by the drivers are relevant in today's world in most countries, not just Egypt alone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18404915575560128935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11462898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6483840900669809707">I read this book two weeks after the protests started in Egypt in early 2011 and the 58 short taxi driver stories put a modern country I know little about into perspective. Through these experiences the reader can understand how all of the animosities and frustrations of common, everyday Egyptians toward their government ran by Mubarak for 30 years have festered, built up, and finally boiled over. Unlike many government officials sitting in their office, the taxi drivers drive the streets all da</span>
  <span id="freeText6483840900669809707" style="display:none">I read this book two weeks after the protests started in Egypt in early 2011 and the 58 short taxi driver stories put a modern country I know little about into perspective. Through these experiences the reader can understand how all of the animosities and frustrations of common, everyday Egyptians toward their government ran by Mubarak for 30 years have festered, built up, and finally boiled over. Unlike many government officials sitting in their office, the taxi drivers drive the streets all day long and can feel the pulse of the general populace and has a true sense where a nation is going. Each short dialogue speaks volumes.<br /><br />Whenever I get inside a taxi, whether it is in NYC, DC, Las Vegas, or where ever, it is interesting to learn about the person driving the car. More often than not I find drivers are immigrants putting in the long hours trying to turn a profit so that they can provide a better life for their family. Compassion and understanding must be felt for the taxi drivers in Egypt who only want the same thing but find it difficult because of all the corruption and predatory lending by banks.<br /><br />This compilation of short stories broadcasts the injustices and deficiencies of the Egyptian system to the world not to cause trouble, but to stimulate conversation and and internal dialogue between the Egyptian people and their government to bring about meaningful change. Four years has passed since the publishing of Khaled Al Khamissi's Taxi and the problems then are still here. I hope the protests result in a new government with new reforms and can listen to the people so that Egyptian society will benefit.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6483840900669809707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781906300029" href="/book/show/2091415.Taxi">review of another edition</a>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Amaani by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5377127-antastesia">Antastesia</a>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46916873-amaani?shelf=translated-lit">translated-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46916873-amaani?shelf=egyptian-lit">egyptian-lit</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1626221022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8706570123838719958">‘Taxi’ by Khaled AlKhamissi<br/><br/>The collection of small snippets of the conversations and happenings of taxi drivers in Cairo creates a wider portrayal of Egyptian society. Revealing political sentiments (although the author has to be extremely careful about what he actually says and how he says – there are numerous footnotes that the author has added to reinforce his neutrality towards different political opinions in the book). We are given characters who feel immense love for Egypt, and others who </span>
  <span id="freeText8706570123838719958" style="display:none">‘Taxi’ by Khaled AlKhamissi<br /><br />The collection of small snippets of the conversations and happenings of taxi drivers in Cairo creates a wider portrayal of Egyptian society. Revealing political sentiments (although the author has to be extremely careful about what he actually says and how he says – there are numerous footnotes that the author has added to reinforce his neutrality towards different political opinions in the book). We are given characters who feel immense love for Egypt, and others who only dream to leave it. We meet characters who see a future in Egypt, and others that have lost all hope in themselves and the system. The ‘system’ is what brings all of these taxi drivers together – they are joint in the fact that they are victims of the system. It is clear that these short stories are the calm before the storm. There are bursts of anger that pop out from the stories, but there is an overwhelming sense of a building atmosphere that will soon explode into the Arab Spring. AlKhamissi does a clever thing by showing the uprising to be a result of everyone’s anger (i.e. Not just students, or intellectuals), it was the revolution of the people, and even the taxi driver had something to say.<br /><br />The two short stories that stood out the most were number nine and number forty-two.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8706570123838719958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

They constitute a very poor social class, so they serve as a representation long-suffering people in Egypt. The language in which this book is presented is the very colloquial, natural and even obscene in some parts used by taxi drivers, so the book is a mirror that reflects much about the Egyptian street and the Egyptian people.</span>

Yes I'd heard about them, but reading about it in details, listening to the average man's tales and stories.. despite a lot of details obviously being omitted for political reasons/ censorship .. it was a True eye opener. I couldn't put the book down.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14672154980636718954">This is an interesting idea but without any sort of prior knowledge of Egypt’s politics it didn’t mean much to me. The chapters where the taxi drivers talked about their personal lives meant more, some themes are universal, having enough money, dealing with bureaucracy and educating their children. But there was a lot of politics. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1288893902" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1288893902" />
    <a title="Elisabeth Sepulveda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15090296-elisabeth-sepulveda"><img alt="Elisabeth Sepulveda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430954665p2/15090296.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1288893902?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elisabeth Sepulveda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elisabeth Sepulveda" href="/user/show/15090296-elisabeth-sepulveda">Elisabeth Sepulveda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15090296-elisabeth-sepulveda?shelf=humanity">humanity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15090296-elisabeth-sepulveda?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1288893902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12474879575077128786"><br/>While taxis are present in many locations, and a walk through New York City brings hundreds into view, the descriptions in “Taxi” by al-Khamissi reflect interesting aspects of Egyptian culture, particularly in the stories and responses of the drivers themselves.<br/><br/>Most of the individuals driving the taxis do so, not out of personal interest in the occupation, but through economic pressure. They need to put food on the table, and without an alternative for work, begin driving routes. As one driver s</span>
  <span id="freeText12474879575077128786" style="display:none"><br />While taxis are present in many locations, and a walk through New York City brings hundreds into view, the descriptions in “Taxi” by al-Khamissi reflect interesting aspects of Egyptian culture, particularly in the stories and responses of the drivers themselves.<br /><br />Most of the individuals driving the taxis do so, not out of personal interest in the occupation, but through economic pressure. They need to put food on the table, and without an alternative for work, begin driving routes. As one driver said, “It’s impossible for anyone in Egypt to make do with his salary. Because how much are salaries? From 300 to 600 pounds and no more than that. And that’s not enough. So what’s the answer? Either we steal or take bribes or work all day. I work (p.77) Another driver—a graduate of the Faculty of Commerce— did not identify as taxi driver, but supplemented his work at the pharmaceutical company for the extra income (76). Still another poured his energies into the stock exchange while continuing to drive a taxi out of personal need (57). <br />While driving taxis from economic necessity is not unique to Egypt, the ways in which this need appears to be frustrated by government regulation is emphasized throughout the book. Indeed, “one of the most popular pastimes for the Cairo taxi driver is cursing the Interior Ministry, and respecting, revering and venerating it at the same time, because they – the drivers and the Interior Ministry’s traffic department – are always out on the streets” (6-7) Often, this cursing results from a feeling of victimization, as the driver feels forced into a state of need as a means of state control and coercion. One driver said, “They planted hunger in the belly of Egyptian, a terror that made everyone look out for himself or say ‘Why should I make it my problem?’” (11). Another driver said, “Frankly the government does everything it can to turn us into beggars or criminals. You feel they’re making a big effort to ruin us and our families, and don’t forget that the taxi drivers in Cairo are no small number” (117). While the government’s behavior may seem irrational, it is, in fact, quite strategic. When drivers—and the people of Egypt as whole—are focused on immediate, personal necessity, a move towards unified demand of structural change is more difficult.<br /><br />Indeed, some of the examples of coercion cited in the book relate directly to the taxis. For instance, when the Interior Ministry began requiring seatbelts—what was previously a luxury item in cabs—a driver said “The whole story was business on business. The big guys imported seatbelts and sold them and made millions” (23). Creating such business conditions ensures a more profitable and stable future for the government, while brewing resentment in the drivers. And yet, often the only course of action for these drivers is passive complaint. One driver said, “I’d really like to tell the minister of information we’re a thousand times cleverer than him and understand the world a hundred times better than him. But where would I see the minister of information to tell him that? What do you say I send him a telegram? Or might they arrest me if I sent him a telegram? What do I care that this used to be our country? Now it’s their country and they do what they like with it! I’d better just stick to the taxi.” (108) In this way, drivers are subscripted to simply accept what is, navigating the roads which, ironically, are often named after political figures, serving as a continual reminder of who ultimately establishes the rules and framework for these drivers to operate in (46).<br /><br />If I were to visit Egypt, I would be interested in driving through the streets in a taxi, both to observe the topography and to converse with the driver. I come from a small town in Maine where taxis are never used, however my experiences in cities with public transportation confirm that many of the operators of the buses and trains I’ve taken would probably agree with the driver that said “The world is now fish eat fish, big and small, snatching and grabbing”, as it is a “constant struggle to make a living” (114, 6). What I would be most interested in when visiting Egypt, then, beyond a reaction to the necessity of making a living, is whether the drivers attribute their conditions to the government, specifically in relation to government agents using coercive regulation to create economic need and keep rebellion at bay. Taxis are not the only place to glean this understanding, and I would certainly seek to converse with many different people for their perspectives. However, in sharing in this culture of transportation, I feel that I would be able to connect the sites I observe with the interpretation of the driver in a way that would shape my image of the city with greater clarity and understanding.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12474879575077128786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1288893902'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F2508854._">Like</a></span>
&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a href="/review/show/1288893902?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>
    </div>


    <div id='comments_for_review_1288893902' style='display: none;'>

<div id="review_1225396000" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1225396000" />
    <a title="Henry Watts" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19752638-henry-watts"><img alt="Henry Watts" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549195459p2/19752638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1225396000?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Watts" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Watts" href="/user/show/19752638-henry-watts">Henry Watts</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781906300029" href="/book/show/2091415.Taxi">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1225396000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10248408465707438563">The accreditation on the front cover that this is ‘the novel that predicted the [Arab] uprising’ is an accurate one. Alkhamissi depicts an Egyptian society, disparate, but more or less unified in its frustration for Hosni Mubarak’s 25-year-long state monopoly before his downfall in 2011. It is a recording from the backseats of Cairo’s taxis of the whispers of a nascent revolution – the moment that Egypt’s cynicism began to turn much more visceral.<br/><br/>But the novel is prescient not only for revealing</span>
  <span id="freeText10248408465707438563" style="display:none">The accreditation on the front cover that this is ‘the novel that predicted the [Arab] uprising’ is an accurate one. Alkhamissi depicts an Egyptian society, disparate, but more or less unified in its frustration for Hosni Mubarak’s 25-year-long state monopoly before his downfall in 2011. It is a recording from the backseats of Cairo’s taxis of the whispers of a nascent revolution – the moment that Egypt’s cynicism began to turn much more visceral.<br /><br />But the novel is prescient not only for revealing signs of the imminent democratic uprising (the ‘Arab Spring’) but also of the ‘Islamist Winter’ that would follow.<br /><br />A number of the 58 fictitious monologues that comprise Alkhamissi’s novel suggest varyingly a resonant public sympathy in Egypt towards a greater role for Islam in governance, bolstered with Islamic/ Islamist prescriptions from a cross-section of Cairo’s Muslim cab drivers to a variety of problems.<br /><br />“There would be no bribery or corruption” says one, “if everyone in the country sat and looked at the surface of the Nile and read the word of God.” “We’ve tried everything else” says another – monarchy, socialism, military dictatorship, capitalism, and “it’s still no good”. “Why don’t we try the Brotherhood and maybe they will work out, who knows?”<br /><br />Although Alkhamissi does not explicitly state his own position on these prescriptions, the picture he paints of Egypt – a richly diverse society with wide ranging identities, ideologies and interests – is such to suggest it would be much ill-disposed to the establishment of the Salafi-sympathasing Islamist theocracy that eventuated after the uprising. It is unlikely that many of the eventual harbingers of this theocracy, the Muslim Brotherhood, had read Alkhamissi’s rich ethnography before assuming office, but it is fair to suppose that even without doing so they would have had an understanding of Egypt’s complex socio-religious makeup – before ultimately deciding to trample on it.<br /><br />Liberal secularist, Baha’ais and Coptic Christians feature in Alkhamissi’s tribute to Egypt’s people but were all absent in the making of Egypt’s future. Morsi and the Brotherhood decided instead to pack the committee charged with drafting the constitution with the ultra-conservative Salafis and patsy Islamist legislators.<br /><br />Though Morsi and the Brotherhood – having won presidential and parliamentary elections, respectively – had democratic (electoral) sanction, what they had done, ultimately, was turn Egypt’s popular revolution into a tyranny of the majority, invoking thinly veiled democratic justifications to impede real democratic consolidation – smothering a civil society that had only just found its voice from the dark days of Mubarak..<br /><br />Unfortunately for Egypt, and the future of civilisation, the picture is much bleaker today. If Alkhamissi was to write a second edition of ‘Taxi’, he would now find his Cairo host to many new troubled faces – including an increasingly large population of Syrian and Iraqis heading in to flee the same jihadist battle cry that many young and disillusioned Egyptians now clamour to go and join.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10248408465707438563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_266540621" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/266540621" />
    <a title="Steve Porter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3996367-steve-porter"><img alt="Steve Porter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1279023302p2/3996367.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/266540621?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve Porter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve Porter" href="/user/show/3996367-steve-porter">Steve Porter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789992178713" href="/book/show/11740407-taxi">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer266540621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4820911239341134151">This is the kind of book I really love – informative fiction that makes you feel you know a place better than you would had you read a historical account or travel guide. Taxi is based around 58 conversations with cab drivers in Cairo and throughout the reader is given an insight into the minds of the people. The English translation is by Jonathan Wright and it reads very well.<br/><br/>The overriding impression you get of the people is that they have a serious mistrust of anything to do with the Egyptian</span>
  <span id="freeText4820911239341134151" style="display:none">This is the kind of book I really love – informative fiction that makes you feel you know a place better than you would had you read a historical account or travel guide. Taxi is based around 58 conversations with cab drivers in Cairo and throughout the reader is given an insight into the minds of the people. The English translation is by Jonathan Wright and it reads very well.<br /><br />The overriding impression you get of the people is that they have a serious mistrust of anything to do with the Egyptian state. The timing of the book is important given that it was written and published just shortly before the fall of Mubarak’s regime. Since this is a work of fiction, it must be acknowledged that this is ultimately the way one man has chosen to represent his country – author Khaled Alkhamissi – but it appears that discontent had been simmering for a long time before the patience and stoicism of many in Egypt finally broke last year. Hearsay and corruption are equally rife and, although religion plays its part in the uncertain revolution the country is still in the midst of, clearly many people are more concerned with pressing everyday problems. In short, a wonderful insight into the mindset of people in Cairo and their preoccupations.<br /></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_672289459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/672289459" />
    <a title="jhldjfau" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4982440-jhldjfau"><img alt="jhldjfau" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/images/nophoto/user/c_111x148.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/672289459?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jhldjfau" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jhldjfau" href="/user/show/4982440-jhldjfau">jhldjfau</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781906300029" href="/book/show/2091415.Taxi">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer672289459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer108396414981062022">Everyone knows that the real way to get to know any country is to hail a cab, jump in, and let the driver rant and rave at will. This is the starting premise of Taxi, by Khaled Alkhamissi, whose narrator in the novel we never come to know. Instead, the central character relays the conversations, verbatim, that he has with any number of taxi drivers in Cairo over the course of perhaps a year. Published in 2006 prior to the Arab spring, it has been described by critics as "The novel that predicted</span>
  <span id="freeText108396414981062022" style="display:none">Everyone knows that the real way to get to know any country is to hail a cab, jump in, and let the driver rant and rave at will. This is the starting premise of Taxi, by Khaled Alkhamissi, whose narrator in the novel we never come to know. Instead, the central character relays the conversations, verbatim, that he has with any number of taxi drivers in Cairo over the course of perhaps a year. Published in 2006 prior to the Arab spring, it has been described by critics as "The novel that predicted the uprising". <br /><br />It lays out a myriad of views and experiences of common people living in Egypt, their daily realities, challenges and hopes in life. It is an overtly political novel, although the characters themselves are not inherently politicised. They're just trying to get on in life; to work and provide for their families, to educate their children and provide them with a future that is secure. But they're thwarted at every turn, and everybody has a different opinion about who is to blame and what should be done about it.<br /><br />The novel is dedicated "to the life that dwells in the words of simple people", and "simple" though those people may be, I found this book profoundly insightful and more enlightening than the nightly news.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="108396414981062022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IQzJTqBixjgF/xeQM82UpOKyaPeFzPVglOd2/9PlsigTDgFe18QoVxFl5wJy0+CNLP0CEB+WALXPhV/fPYtNEg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LV6QdvDNutYqegHhpR5UKyngzbXNm+X0cH/+UMYZqW4fXFhmh2tUuT7g8XPkACAC56+nUlfBECErHddwKHdWVA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3158133.Khaled_Al_Khamissi">About Khaled Al Khamissi</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3158133.Khaled_Al_Khamissi"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1543317505p3/3158133.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6694665491432242330">Khaled Al Khamissi was born to Egyptian actress Faten El Choubachi and poet Abdel Rahman Al Khamissi Lenin Peace Prize (1979) is an Egyptian novelist, columnist, lecturer, writer and cultural activist. <br/><br/>Upon his mother's death when he was five, Al Khamissi was raised by his maternal grandfather Moufid El Choubachi, also a poet, writer and critic. He grew up in a book-lined home, where politics, lit</span>
  <span id="freeText6694665491432242330" style="display:none">Khaled Al Khamissi was born to Egyptian actress Faten El Choubachi and poet Abdel Rahman Al Khamissi Lenin Peace Prize (1979) is an Egyptian novelist, columnist, lecturer, writer and cultural activist. <br /><br />Upon his mother's death when he was five, Al Khamissi was raised by his maternal grandfather Moufid El Choubachi, also a poet, writer and critic. He grew up in a book-lined home, where politics, literature and art were constantly discussed.<br /><br />Al Khamissi obtained a degree in Political Science from Cairo University in 1984 and a Master's degree in International Politics from the Sorbonne in 1987. His two fiction works, Taxi and Noah’s Ark, have provided Arabic and non-Arabic readers with insight into Egyptian society in the last decade. His first non fiction book: 2011 was published in 2014. As a columnist, his articles in Egypt and abroad show a blend of his background as a political analyst and fiction writer.<br /><br />He has been a jury member in a number of film festivals, and Chairman of the board of the Greater Cairo public Library since 2014. He regularly delivers lectures in international universities and book fairs on literature and film.<br /><br />A believer that cultural progress is the necessary precursor to social change, following the Egyptian revolution of 2011, Al Khamissi set up Doum , a cultural foundation designed to promote critical thinking. Founder and president of story telling festival in Qena Founder and president of literary festival in Mansoura.<br /><br />His novels have been translated into 16 languages including: English, French, Italian, Spanish, German, Malaysian, Korean, Polish, Greek, Kurdish and others.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6694665491432242330" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
